Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn on Wasl and Fasl in Iqrar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on connected vs disconnected statements in iqrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the effect of connecting (wasl) versus disconnecting (fasl) a qualifying statement in iqrar, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
In iqrar, connecting a qualification can change the ruling: Sahibayn accept it as explanatory, Abu Hanifa rejects it as retraction.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: when a person acknowledges a debt but immediately qualifies it by claiming non-receipt of the item, does the qualification affect the obligation? The key distinction is whether the qualification is connected (wasl) or disconnected (fasl). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) distinguished between the two, while Abu Hanifa rejected any qualification as a retraction. Understanding this helps clarify the conditions under which an acknowledgment remains binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and key terms
The scenario: a person says 'I owe you 1,000 dirhams from the price of a slave' and adds 'but I did not receive the slave.' The terms wasl (connected) and fasl (disconnected) refer to whether the addition is made immediately without pause or after a separation. Note also the cases of wine/pork.
Wasl means the qualification is uttered in the same breath; fasl means there is a temporal or verbal gap.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
According to Abu Hanifa, the debt is binding and the claim of non-receipt is not accepted whether connected or disconnected. He considers any such qualification a retraction (ruju') because the beginning of the speech indicates obligation.
Abu Hanifa's rule is strict: once an obligation is acknowledged, any subsequent denial or qualification is invalid regardless of how it is phrased.
3
State the position of the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn distinguish: if the qualification is connected (wasl), the muqir is believed and nothing is binding; if disconnected (fasl), he is not believed. They argue that the connected form shows the muqir never intended an unconditional obligation, and receipt is necessary for bindingness.
For them, the entire utterance is a single speech act; the qualification clarifies the intention.
4
Analyze the reasoning of each side
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the beginning (obligation) and end (denial) contradict, and any contradiction is a retraction. The Sahibayn reason: if the claimant agrees on the cause (sale), the obligation is not confirmed without receipt; if he denies the cause, then the qualification is a 'changing clarification' (bayan mughayyir) valid only if connected. They analogize to ta'liq (if Allah wills) but note the difference: ta'liq suspends, while here it is ibtal (nullification).
The dispute hinges on whether the qualification is seen as part of the original statement or as a separate retraction.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Practical consequence: According to Sahibayn, a person can escape liability by connecting the qualification (e.g., 'I owe you but I didn't receive it' in one breath). According to Abu Hanifa, the debt remains binding regardless. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa.
This shows how a procedural difference in utterance can have a substantive effect on legal obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَالَ أَبُويُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدُ إِنْ وَصَلَ صُدِقَ وَلَمْ يَلْزَمُهُ شَيْءٌ وَإِنْ فَصَلَ لَمْ يُصَدَّقْ إِذَا انْكَرَ الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ أَنْ يَكُونَ ذَالِكَ مِنْ ثَمَن عَبْدِ وَإِنْ أَقَرَّ انَّهُ بَاعَهُ مَتَاعًا فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الْمُقَرُّ وَوَجْهُ ذَالِكَ أَنَّهُ أَقَرَّ بِوُجُوبِ الْمَالَ عَلَيْهِ وَبَيَّنَ سَبَبًا وَهُوَ الْبَيْعُ فَإِنْ وَافَقَهُ الطَّالِبُ فِي السَّبَبِ وَبَهِ لَا يَتَأَكَدُ الْوُجُوبُ إِلَّا بِالْقَبْضِ وَالْمُقَرُّ يُنْكِرُهُ فَيَكُونُ الْقَوْلُ قَوْلُهُ وَإِنْ كَذَّبَه فِي السَّبَبِ كَانَ هَذَا مِنَ الْمُقَرِ بَيَانًا مُغَيّرًا لِاَنَّ صَدْرَ كَلَامِهِ لِلْوُجُوبِ مُطْلَقًا وَآخِرُهُ يَحْتَمِلُ انْتِفَاءَهُ عَلَى إِعْتِبَارِ عَدَمِ الْقَبْضِ وَالْمُغِيرِ يَصِحُ مَوْصُولًا لَا مَفْصُولًا وَلَوْ قَالَ إِبْتَعَتْ مِنْهُ عَيْنًا إِلَّا إِنِّي لَمْ أَقْبِضْهُ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُهُ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ مِنْ ضُرُورَةِ الْبَيْعِ الْقَبْضِ بِخِلَافِ الْإِقْرَارِ بِوُجُوبِ الثَمَنِ قَالَ وَكَذَا لَوْ قَالَ مِنْ ثَمَنِ خَمْرٍ أَوْ خِنْزِيرٍ وَمَعْنَى الْمَسْأَلَةِ إِذَا قَالَ لِفُلَانِ عَلَيَّ اَلَفُ دِرْهَم مِنْ ثَمَنِ الْخَمْرِ أَوِ الْخِنْزِيرِ لَزِمَهُ الْأَلْفُ وَلَمْ يُقْبَل تَفْسِيرُهُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةٌ وَصَلَ أَمْ فَصَلَ لِأَنَّهُ رُجُوعٌ لِأَنَّ ثَمَنَ الْخَمْرِ وَالْخِنْزِيرِ لَا يَكُونُ وَاحِبًا وَأَوَّلُ كَلَامِهِ لِلْوُجُوبِ وَقَالَا إِذَا وَصَلَ لَا يَلْزَمُهُ شَيْءٌ لِأَنَّهُ بَيَّنَ بِآخِرِ كَلَامِهِ أَنَّهُ مَا أَرَادَ بِهِ الْإِيجَابَ وَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا قَالَ فِي آخِرِهِ إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ قُلْنَا ذَالِكَ تَعْلِيْقٌ وَهَذَا إِبْطَالٌ
And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: If he connected (the statement), he is believed and nothing is binding upon him; and if he disconnected, he is not believed, when the person acknowledged to denies that it was from the price of a slave. And if he acknowledges that he sold him merchandise, then the word is the word of the muqir. And the reasoning of that is that he acknowledged the obligation of property upon himself and explained a cause, which is the sale. If the claimant agrees with him on the cause, the obligation is not confirmed except by receipt, and the muqir denies it, so his word is taken. And if he (the claimant) belies him in the cause, then this from the muqir is a changing clarification because the beginning of his speech is for obligation absolutely, and the end of it may imply its negation due to consideration of non-receipt, and a change is valid when connected but not when disconnected. And if he said 'I bought from him a specific item except that I did not receive it,' then his word is taken by consensus, because receipt is not a necessary consequence of sale, unlike the acknowledgment of the obligation of the price. He said: And similarly if he said 'from the price of wine or pork.' The meaning of the issue is that if he said 'I owe so-and-so a thousand dirhams from the price of wine or pork,' the thousand is binding upon him and his explanation is not accepted according to Abu Hanifa, whether connected or disconnected, because it is a retraction, for the price of wine and pork is not obligatory, and the beginning of his speech is for obligation. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If connected, nothing is binding upon him, because he clarified by the end of his speech that he did not intend obligation by it, and it becomes like when he says at the end 'if Allah wills.' We said: That is ta'liq (suspension) and this is ibtal (nullification).
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the validity of a muqir's statement when he qualifies his acknowledgment with a claim of non-receipt. The scenario: a person acknowledges a debt of 1,000 dirhams 'from the price of a slave' and then adds that he did not receive the slave. According to Abu Hanifa, as stated earlier, this is always a retraction and invalid regardless of connection. The Sahibayn differentiate: if the statement is connected (mawsul), the muqir is believed and nothing is binding because the connection indicates that the entire utterance is explanatory, and the obligation is not established without receipt. If disconnected (fasl), he is not believed because it appears as a retraction. The Sahibayn argue that the acknowledgment together with the explanation of the cause (sale) is a single statement; if the claimant agrees on the cause but the muqir denies receipt, the muqir's word is taken because receipt is necessary for obligation to become binding. If the claimant denies the cause itself, then the muqir's statement becomes a 'changing clarification' (bayan mughayyir) which is valid only if connected. The passage then draws an analogy to a sale where receipt is not necessary, and to the case of acknowledging a debt from the price of wine or pork, which are invalid causes. Abu Hanifa applies the same rule: the acknowledgment is binding and any explanation is retraction. The Sahibayn say if connected, it is not binding because it shows no intention of obligation. The final line distinguishes between ta'liq (suspension) and ibtal (nullification).

Key Terms
وصل (wasl): Connecting the qualifying statement (e.g., 'I did not receive it') immediately after the acknowledgment without separation.
فصل (fasl): Disconnecting or separating the qualifying statement from the acknowledgment by time or another utterance.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the obligation on a condition, like saying 'if Allah wills', which is valid and prevents the obligation from being immediate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, if a person says 'I owe you 1,000 dirhams from the price of a slave – but I did not receive the slave' in a single connected sentence, what is the ruling?
A) The debt is binding and the statement is not accepted.
B) The debt is not binding because the statement is connected and explains the intention.
C) The debt is binding only if the claimant agrees to the cause.
D) The ruling is the same as if it were disconnected.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, whether a qualifying statement in iqrar changes the ruling depends on connection (wasl vs fasl), with Abu Hanifa's stricter position being the relied-upon view.
